Effect of Khiyar on Possession and Validity in Coin Sales
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar and possession in coin sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sale of coins with khiyar is valid according to the Sahibayn and when it is invalid, and apply the ruling to both-sided and one-sided khiyar.

Core Idea
Khiyar on both sides invalidates possession; one-sided khiyar is valid according to the Sahibayn but invalid according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson addresses a specific point of contention regarding coin sales: how does the condition of option (khiyar) affect the validity of the sale and the effectiveness of possession (qabd)? The passage from al-Muhit presents the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether khiyar prevents valid possession, which in turn determines the sale's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: khiyar in a coin sale
Two parties exchange coins that are the subject of a sale, but they stipulate an option (khiyar) for both sides or for one side. The question is whether the possession (qabd) is valid given the option.
Khiyar here is the right to rescind the sale within a specified period.
2
State the ruling for khiyar on both sides
If both parties have khiyar, the sale is invalid (fasid) because the option prevents valid possession in either party. The condition of khiyar contradicts the immediate transfer of ownership required for a valid sale.
This is agreed upon by all three imams.
3
State the Sahibayn's view on one-sided khiyar
If only one party has khiyar (e.g., the buyer), the sale is valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. They argue that the option does not prevent ownership from being established in the other party, so the required possession is found in the other side.
They consider that the party without khiyar can take valid possession and ownership.
4
State Abu Hanifa's view on one-sided khiyar
Abu Hanifa holds that the sale is invalid even if only one party has khiyar. He argues that khiyar affects both sides because it suspends the finality of the transaction, thereby preventing valid possession in either party.
For Abu Hanifa, the mere existence of a khiyar condition taints the entire possession.
5
Note the exception: sale of a specific coin for two coins with khiyar
The passage concludes with a specific case: selling one specific fils for two specific fils with khiyar is valid. This is an exception because the subject and price are identified, and khiyar is allowed.
This is consistent with the general permissibility of khiyar in non-currency specific sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms

Checkpoint
In a coin sale where both the buyer and seller have an option to cancel, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The sale is valid if possession is taken.
B) The sale is invalid (fasid).
C) The sale is valid only if one party waives the option.
D) The sale is suspended until the option expires.

Key Takeaway
One-sided khiyar is valid according to the mufta bihi view; both-sided khiyar invalidates the sale.

Selling Meat for a Live Animal: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of bartering meat for a live animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on selling meat for a live animal and apply the key illa underlying their disagreement.

Core Idea
Meat is weighed and a live animal is not; Hanafis disagree whether this difference permits barter without equality.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a practical transaction: exchanging meat for a live animal. While the sale appears straightforward, the Hanafi school records an internal dispute between its three primary imams. The disagreement hinges on whether the items are of the same genus or not, and whether uncertainty (gharar) in weight invalidates the sale. In this lesson, we will dissect each position and the analogies used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit the sale unconditionally; Muhammad permits it only if the separated meat is more than the meat on the live animal.
Muhammad's condition ensures the excess meat compensates for the offal (saqt).
2
Locate the key proof
Muhammad analogizes to selling a weighed item (meat) for a non-weighed item (live animal), like selling a sword for iron. Abu Hanifa analogizes to selling sesame oil (hal) for sesame seeds, where oil can be distinguished.
The sword/iron analogy is contested because both sword and iron are not weighed, but meat is weighed.
3
Analyze the illa
The effective cause is that the live animal is not ribawi, so equality is not required; the potential gharar is acceptable because the meat weight can be estimated.
Offal (saqt) is considered a separate category, not subject to ribawi rules.
4
Note the unanimous exception
Selling a slaughtered sheep for a live sheep is permissible unanimously, as both are not weighed.
The slaughtered sheep is treated like a live animal.
5
Apply to related cases
The same logic applies to selling a sheep with milk or wool for their produce, with conditions about excess.
Analogous to selling oil for olives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (واللحم بالحيوان) أي وصح بيع اللحم بالحيوان عند أبي حنيفة وعند أبي يوسف وقال محمد: لا يجوز إذا كان من جنسه إلا إذا كان اللحم المفرز أكثر من اللحم الذي في الحيوان ليكون اللحم بمقابلة ما فيه والباقي من اللحم بمقابله السقط - وهو بفتحتين - ما لا ينطلق عليه اسم اللحم كالجلد والكرش والأمعاء والطحال وصار كالحل - وهو بالمهملة - دهن السمسم ولهما أنه باع الموزون بما ليس بموزون فصار كبيع السيف بالحديد لأن الحيوان لا يوزن عادة ولا يمكن معرفة ثقله بالوزن بخلاف تلك المسألة لأن الوزن في الحل يعرف قدر الدهن إذا ميز. وذكر الشارح: وإنما لا أحدهما بالآخر نسيئة لأن المتأخر منهما لا يمكن ضبطه لا لأنها جنس واحد ألا ترى بيع أنه لا يجوز ذلك إذا بيع بغيره من خلاف الجنس أيضاً .اهـ. ولو باع شاة مذبوحة بشاة حية يجوز عند الكل وعلى هذا شاتان مذبوحتان غير مسلوختين بشاة مذبوحة لم تسلخ يجوز . وفي شرح الطحاوي : لو كانت الشاة مذبوحة غير مسلوخة فاشتراها بلحم الشاة فالجواب في قولهم جميعاً كما قال محمد، وأراد بغير المسلوخة غير المفصولة عن السقط. وفي الحاوي: لو باع شاة في ضرعها لبن بجنس لبنها فهو على الاختلاف الذي في اللحم . قوله : (والكرباس بالقطن وكذا بالغزل كيفما كان أي صح لاختلافهما جنساً لأن الثوب لا ينقض ليعود غزلاً يجوز . الرملي : وسيأتي مزيد بحث في أحكام الفلوس من كتاب الصرف. قوله : (وإن افترقا لا عن قبض أحدهما جاز) قال الرملي: صوابه لا يجوز». قوله : (وفي الحاوي لو باع شاة الخ) قال في النهر : أن أو قطناً، والكرباس الثياب من اللحم والجمع كرابيس وإليها ينسب الإمام المحبوبي باعتبار بيعها. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو باع القطن المحلوج بغزل فإنه يجوز كيفما كان لاختلاف الجنس وهو قول محمد. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يجوز إلا متساوياً. وقول محمد أظهر. وفي الحاوي: وهو الأصح ولو باع المحلوج بغير المحلوج جاز إذا علم أن الخالص أكثر مما في الآخر، وإن كان لا يدري لا يجوز وكذا لو باع القطن غير المحلوج بحب القطن فلا بد يكون الحب الخالص أكثر من الحب الذي في القطن حتى يكون قدره مقابلاً به والزائد بالقطن وكذا لو باع شاة على ظهرها صوف أو في ضرعها لبن بصوف أو لبن يشترط أن يكون الصوف أو اللبن أكثر مما على الشاة لما ذكرنا من المعنى وهو نظير بيع الزيت بالزيتون
His saying: '(Meat for a live animal)' means that the sale of meat for a live animal is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad said: It is not permissible if it is of the same genus, unless the separated meat is more than the meat in the animal, so that the meat is in exchange for what is in it, and the remainder of the meat is in exchange for the rest (saqt) - which is, with two fathas, that which the name 'meat' does not apply to, like skin, stomach, intestines, and spleen. It is like hal (with an important letter: sesame oil). Their proof (for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) is that he sold a weighed item for a non-weighed item, so it is like selling a sword for iron, because a live animal is not usually weighed and its weight cannot be known by weighing, unlike that issue because the oil's weight is known when it is distinguished. And the commentator mentioned: They do not allow one for the other on credit because the later one cannot be controlled, not because they are of the same genus. Do you not see that this sale is not permissible if sold with something of a different genus as well? End quote. If someone sells a slaughtered sheep for a live sheep, it is permissible according to all. Similarly, two slaughtered but not skinned sheep for one slaughtered but not skinned sheep is permissible. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If the sheep is slaughtered but not skinned, and he buys it for the meat of a sheep, the answer in all their statements is as Muhammad said; and by 'not skinned' he means not separated from the offal. In al-Hawi: If someone sells a sheep with milk in its udder for milk of its own kind, it is based on the difference regarding meat. His saying: '(and cloth for cotton, and likewise for yarn, however it may be)' means it is valid because they differ in genus, because a garment is not undone to become yarn again. It is permissible. al-Ramli: And a further discussion of the rulings of coins will come from the book of Sarf. His saying: (And if they separate without one of them taking possession, it is permissible) al-Ramli said: The correct wording is 'it is not permissible'. His saying: (And in al-Hawi, if someone sells a sheep...) al-Nahr said: That, or cotton, and al-karbas is garments made of meat (?) and the plural is karabis, and to it is attributed al-Imam al-Mahbubi with consideration of its sale. And the author indicated that if someone sells carded cotton for yarn, it is permissible however it may be because of difference in genus; this is the saying of Muhammad. And Abu Yusuf said: It is not permissible unless equal. And the saying of Muhammad is more apparent. In al-Hawi: It is the most correct. And if someone sells carded cotton for uncarded cotton, it is permissible if he knows that the pure (cotton) is more than what is in the other; if he does not know, it is not permissible. Likewise, if someone sells uncarded cotton for cotton seeds, it is necessary that the pure seeds be more than the seeds in the cotton, so that the amount corresponds to it, and the excess for the cotton. And likewise, if someone sells a sheep with wool on its back or milk in its udder for wool or milk, it is a condition that the wool or milk be more than what is on the sheep, due to the meaning we mentioned; and it is similar to selling oil for olives.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two related rulings from the matn: (1) selling meat for a live animal, and (2) selling cloth (karbas) for cotton or yarn. The first ruling involves an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit selling meat for a live animal (regardless of equality), while Muhammad prohibits it unless the separated meat is greater than the meat on the animal, so that the excess meat corresponds to the offal (saqt). The analogy is to sesame oil (hal): selling oil for sesame seeds is permissible because the oil can be distinguished. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (together?) reason that it is a sale of a weighed item (meat) for a non-weighed item (live animal), which is invalid due to uncertainty in weight. However, Abu Hanifa allows it by analogy to selling a sword for iron, where both are of the same genus? The passage also mentions that selling a slaughtered sheep for a live sheep is unanimously permissible. The second ruling on cloth/cotton/yarn also has a dispute: Muhammad allows it because they are different genera, but Abu Yusuf requires equality in weight. The commentary further discusses selling carded cotton for uncarded cotton, and selling a sheep with wool or milk for its produce, with conditions about excess. The overall principle is that when two items are of different genera (like meat vs. live animal, cloth vs. cotton), the sale is permissible even without equality, as long as there is no gharar (uncertainty). But within the same genus, equality is required to avoid riba.

Key Terms
السقط (as-saqṭ): The parts of a slaughtered animal that are not considered meat, such as skin, stomach, intestines, spleen.
الحل (al-ḥall): Sesame oil (دهن السمسم). Used as an analogy: selling oil for sesame seeds is allowed because the oil can be distinguished.
الموزون (al-mawzūn): The weighed item (in this context, meat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, when is it permissible to sell meat for a live animal?
A) Always permissible, regardless of quantity.
B) Only if the separated meat is more than the meat on the live animal.
C) Only if they are equal in weight.
D) It is never permissible.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of bartering meat for a live animal depends on the imam's view of genus and gharar; the school's majority view allows it unconditionally.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Cloth for Cotton or Yarn: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of exchanging cloth for cotton or yarn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Muhammad allows selling cloth for cotton or yarn unconditionally, while Abu Yusuf requires equality, and identify the principle of genus change.

Core Idea
Cloth and cotton are different genera; thus exchange without equality is allowed by the preferred view.

The second part of the passage deals with exchanging cloth (karbas) for cotton or yarn. While these items are related as raw material and finished product, the Hanafi school sees them as distinct genera (ajnas). The dispute here is between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, with Muhammad's view being deemed more apparent (azhar). This lesson explores the reasoning and its extensions to carded cotton and sheep with wool or milk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Muhammad permits selling cloth for cotton or yarn unconditionally; Abu Yusuf requires equality in weight.
The matn says 'howsoever it may be' (كيفما كان) indicating Muhammad's unconditional permission.
2
Locate the dalil for Muhammad's view
The text states: 'because they differ in genus, for a garment is not undone to become yarn again.'
Cloth and cotton are thus of different legal categories, so no riba considerations.
3
Note the tarjih
The commentary explicitly states: 'The saying of Muhammad is more apparent (azhar) and it is the most correct (al-asahh).'
Thus the mufta bihi position is Muhammad's.
4
Extend to carded vs uncarded cotton
Selling carded cotton (محلوج) for uncarded cotton requires knowledge that the pure cotton in the carded is more than in the uncarded.
This is because they are the same genus (cotton) but processed differently, so equality is needed for the pure component.
5
Apply to sheep with wool/milk
Selling a sheep with wool or milk for wool or milk follows the same rule: the wool/milk sold must be more than what is on the sheep.
Analogous to selling oil for olives.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two related rulings from the matn: (1) selling meat for a live animal, and (2) selling cloth (karbas) for cotton or yarn. The first ruling involves an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit selling meat for a live animal (regardless of equality), while Muhammad prohibits it unless the separated meat is greater than the meat on the animal, so that the excess meat corresponds to the offal (saqt). The analogy is to sesame oil (hal): selling oil for sesame seeds is permissible because the oil can be distinguished. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (together?) reason that it is a sale of a weighed item (meat) for a non-weighed item (live animal), which is invalid due to uncertainty in weight. However, Abu Hanifa allows it by analogy to selling a sword for iron, where both are of the same genus? The passage also mentions that selling a slaughtered sheep for a live sheep is unanimously permissible. The second ruling on cloth/cotton/yarn also has a dispute: Muhammad allows it because they are different genera, but Abu Yusuf requires equality in weight. The commentary further discusses selling carded cotton for uncarded cotton, and selling a sheep with wool or milk for its produce, with conditions about excess. The overall principle is that when two items are of different genera (like meat vs. live animal, cloth vs. cotton), the sale is permissible even without equality, as long as there is no gharar (uncertainty). But within the same genus, equality is required to avoid riba.

Key Terms
الكرباس (al-karbās): A type of cloth or garment, often made of cotton.
المحلوج (al-muḥallaj): Carded cotton, i.e., cotton that has been cleaned and separated from seeds.
الغزل (al-ghazl): Yarn, the product of spinning fibers.

Ruling on Exchanging Fresh Dates for Dried Dates and Grapes for Raisins
Inter-madhhab dispute on riba in exchange of fresh and dried fruits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa permits exchanging fresh dates for dried dates in equal weight and evaluate the opposing proof based on hadith criticism.

Core Idea
Fresh dates and dried dates are the same genus for Hanafis, so equal exchange is valid—but other schools disagree based on a hadith about decrease upon drying.

This passage presents a classical inter-madhhab dispute on riba al-fadl. Abu Hanifa holds that fresh dates (rutab) and dried dates (tamr) are the same genus, so exchanging them in equal weight is permissible. The majority, including the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), disagree, citing a hadith where the Prophet forbade it because the fresh dates decrease when dried. The discussion hinges on hadith authenticity and the definition of genus.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa rules regarding the exchange of fresh dates for dried dates. He permits it only if equal in weight (mithlan bi-mithl).
This is the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
The majority (including Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and all non-Hanafis) prohibit even equal exchange. Their key proof is the hadith of Sa'd: the Prophet forbade because fresh dates lose weight upon drying, leading to riba.
The opposing hadith is reported by Malik and others.
3
Follow the opponent's proof in detail
Examine the proof (dalil) of the majority. The hadith: 'Does it decrease when it dries? ... No, then.' This indicates that the exchange involves uncertainty and inequality.
The thubut is zanni (ahad hadith), but dalala is clear.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds by calling the narrator Zaid bin 'Ayyash weak, thus the hadith is not authentic enough to overcome the general rule. He also cites another hadith where the Prophet called fresh dates 'tamr', making them the same genus.
This is a classic example of ta'dil wa tajrih in hadith criticism.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi fiqh, one can sell fresh dates for dried dates if equal in weight. In other schools, this transaction is riba and invalid.
This also applies to grapes and raisins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والرطب بالرطب أو بالتمر متماثلاً والعنب بالزبيب أي متماثلاً أيضاً. أما الأول فهو قول أبي حنيفة. وقال الباقون من العلماء ومنهم أبو يوسف ومحمد لا يجوز. وأجمعوا على أن بيع الرطب بالتمر متفاضلاً لا يجوز. ودليل الجماعة لقوله حين سئل عنه أينقص إذا جف؟ فقيل : نعم فقال : لا .إذن. رواه مالك في الموطأ والأربعة في السنن عن زيد بن عياش عن سعد بن أبي وقاص وله أن الرطب تمر لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام حين أهدي إليه رطب : أو كل تمر خيبر هكذا؟ سماه تمراً، وتعقبه في غاية البيان بأن الهدية كانت تمراً وتبعه في البناية بأن الثابت في البخاري أنها تمر ولأن الرطب لو كان تمراً جاز البيع بأول الحديث وهو التمر بالتمر وإن كان غير تمر فبآخره وهو إذا اختلف النوعان «فبيعوا كيف شئتم هكذا استدل الإمام الأعظم حين اجتمع عليه علماء بغداد وكانوا أشداء عليه لمخالفته الخبر. وأجاب عن حديثهم بأن مداره على زيد بن عياش وهو ممن لا يقبل حديثه. وفي الهداية : وهو ضعيف عند النقلة وتعقبه في البناية بأنه ثقة عند النقلة. قال الخطابي : وقد تكلم بعض الناس في إسناد هذا الحديث وقال : زيد بن عياش مجهول وليس كذلك فإن ابن عياش هذا مولى لبني زهرة وقد ذكره مالك في الموطأ وأخرج حديثه مع شدة تحريه في الرجال ونقده وتتبعه لأحوالهم. وقد أخرجه الترمذي وقال حديث حسن صحيح ورواه أحمد في مسنده وابن حبان في صحيحه والحاكم في المستدرك وقال : هذا حديث صحيح لإجماع
His saying: (Fresh dates with fresh dates or with dried dates in equal amounts, and grapes with raisins, also in equal amounts). As for the first, it is the saying of Abu Hanifa. And the rest of the scholars, including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, say it is not permissible. They all agree that selling fresh dates for dried dates with disparity is not permissible. The proof of the group (the majority) is his (the Prophet's) saying when he was asked about it: 'Does it decrease when it dries?' It was said: 'Yes.' He said: 'No, then.' Reported by Malik in al-Muwatta' and the four (Sunan) from Zaid bin 'Ayyash from Sa'd bin Abi Waqqas. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that fresh dates are (the same as) dried dates because of his saying, peace be upon him, when fresh dates were given to him: 'Or are they the dates of Khaybar like this?' He called them dates. And it was objected in Ghayat al-Bayan that the gift was dried dates, and he was followed in al-Binayah that what is confirmed in al-Bukhari is that they were dried dates. And because if fresh dates were (the same as) dried dates, the sale would be permissible by the first part of the hadith (dates for dates), and if they are not dates, then by the latter part, which is when the two types differ: 'Sell however you wish.' Thus argued the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) when the scholars of Baghdad gathered against him and were severe on him for opposing the hadith. He responded to their hadith (the one about decrease) that its chain depends on Zaid bin 'Ayyash, who is not acceptable according to him. In al-Hidayah: He is weak according to the traditionists. And it was objected in al-Binayah that he is trustworthy according to them. Al-Khattabi said: Some people have spoken about the chain of this hadith and said that Zaid bin 'Ayyash is unknown, but it is not so; indeed, this Ibn 'Ayyash is a freed slave of Banu Zuhrah, and Malik mentioned him in al-Muwatta' and narrated his hadith despite his intense scrutiny of narrators and criticism and tracking of their conditions. Al-Tirmidhi narrated it and said it is hasan sahih, and Ahmad narrated it in his Musnad, Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, and al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak, and he said: This hadith is sahih by consensus.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the exchange of fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr) and grapes for raisins, requiring equality (mithlan bi-mithl). The matn states that Abu Hanifa permits the sale of fresh dates for fresh dates or dried dates, and grapes for raisins, provided they are equal in weight. The rest of the scholars, including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (who are Hanafis themselves), do not permit it. The majority's proof is a hadith where the Prophet was asked about selling fresh dates for dried dates; when asked if they decrease upon drying, the answer was yes, and the Prophet said 'No, then' (i.e., it is riba). Abu Hanifa's proof is another hadith where the Prophet called fresh dates 'tamr' (dates), indicating they are the same genus; thus the general rule 'dates for dates' applies, requiring equality. He also argued that if they are different, the second part of the hadith 'sell as you wish' applies. The passage then discusses the authenticity of the hadith used by the majority: Abu Hanifa considered its narrator Zaid bin 'Ayyash weak, but later scholars (like those in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah) consider him trustworthy. Al-Khattabi defends the narration, and al-Tirmidhi and others authenticate it.

Key Terms
الرطب (ar-ruṭab): Fresh dates, moist and not yet dried.
التمر (at-tamr): Dried dates.
زيد بن عياش (Zayd bin ʿAyyāsh): A narrator of the hadith about dates; Abu Hanifa considered him weak, but later scholars authenticated him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Abu Hanifa's main argument for allowing the sale of fresh dates for dried dates in equal weight?
A) The fresh dates are not ribawi items.
B) The hadith of Sa'd bin Abi Waqqas is authentic and decisive.
C) The Prophet called fresh dates 'tamr', making them the same genus.
D) The sale is allowed even with disparity as long as it is hand-to-hand.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's position that rutab and tamr are the same genus leads to permissibility of equal exchange, but the majority opposes based on a hadith about decrease upon drying.

Selling Combed Cotton for Yarn: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Analogical Extensions
Intra-Hanafi dispute and analogical expansion on ribawi barter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on bartering combed cotton for yarn, identify the mufta bihi, and apply the principle of genus difference to analogous mixed transactions.

Core Idea
Combed cotton and yarn are different genera, so barter is valid without equality per Muhammad; Abu Yusuf requires equality.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the permissibility of bartering combed cotton for yarn. The dispute hinges on whether the items are considered different genera (ikhtilaf al-jins) or the same genus, which determines if equality in measure is required. The text also extends the ruling to other mixed transactions involving cotton and sheep, illustrating Hanafi analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Hanafi positions
State what Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf each hold regarding selling combed cotton for yarn. Muhammad permits it regardless of equality; Abu Yusuf requires equal measure.
Muhammad's view is from Zahir al-Riwayah, as is Abu Yusuf's.
2
Understand the legal basis
Muhammad's basis is 'difference of genus' (ikhtilaf al-jins) – combed cotton and yarn are different in genus, so the ribawi requirement of equality does not apply. Abu Yusuf likely treats them as same genus or applies qiyas to same-genus items.
The 'illa for riba is weight or measure, but genus difference overrides.
3
Apply the later tarjih
The author prefers Muhammad's view as 'more apparent' and cites al-Hawi declaring it 'most correct'. The mufta bihi is permissibility without equality.
This tarjih is consistent with the majority of later Hanafi authorities.
4
Extend to analogous cases
The same principle applies to combed-for-uncombed cotton, uncombed cotton for seeds, and sheep with wool/milk for wool/milk. In mixed cases, the pure portion must be greater for validity.
These are applications of qiyas on the oil-for-olives precedent.
5
Recognize the broader principle
In mixed transactions where one commodity contains another (e.g., cotton with seeds, sheep with wool), validity requires that the pure item exceed the impure, ensuring the exchange is not purely ribawi.
The same reasoning applies in other Hanafi fiqh rulings on mixed items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Sales, Chapter of Riba. 221. That or cotton, and the coarse linen cloth made from flesh, plural karabīs, to which Imam al-Maḥbūbī is attributed in consideration of its sale. The author indicated that if one sells combed cotton for yarn, it is permissible regardless of how it is, due to difference of genus, and that is the statement of Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is not permissible unless equal. And Muhammad's statement is more apparent. In al-Ḥāwī: It is the most correct. And if one sells combed [cotton] for uncombed [cotton], it is permissible if he knows that the pure [cotton] is more than what is in the other; if he does not know, it is not permissible. Similarly, if one sells uncombed cotton for cotton seeds, then the pure seeds must be more than the seeds in the cotton, so that their amount corresponds to them and the excess [is in exchange] for the cotton. Similarly, if one sells a sheep with wool on its back or milk in its udder for wool or milk, it is conditioned that the wool or milk be more than what is on the sheep, due to the meaning we mentioned, and it is analogous to selling oil for olives. 6. And what is mentioned in the Sharḥ that if one sells a sheep with wool on its back or milk in its udder for wool or milk, it is conditioned that the wool and milk be more than what is on the sheep. In al-Sirāj: There is no disagreement among them that selling milk for a sheep with milk in its udder is not permissible except on the basis of estimation; so what is in al-Ḥāwī is weak. Regarding his statement: 'And if one sells combed for uncombed it is permissible etc.' al-Ramlī said: In al-Walwālijiyya, selling combed cotton is not permissible except like for like, and the seeds are not considered; similarly selling dates for split dates because the Prophet ﷺ said 'Dates for dates' the hadith without differentiation. And it is, as you see, contradictory to what is here, so ponder, and it is not hidden that what is here is more apparent.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on bartering cotton and sheep products, with an intra-Hanafi dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf. The author begins by explaining that selling combed cotton for yarn is permissible regardless of equality because they are different genera; this is Muhammad's view, while Abu Yusuf requires equality. Muhammad's position is preferred and considered the most correct in al-Ḥāwī. The same principle applies to selling combed for uncombed cotton: if the pure cotton in the uncombed is greater, the sale is valid, with the excess considered in exchange. For cotton seeds, the pure seeds must exceed the seeds in the cotton. The ruling for sheep is analogous: selling a sheep with wool or milk for wool or milk requires that the wool or milk be more than that on the sheep. The margin note clarifies that some later sources disagree, but the author asserts that the view presented here (Muhammad's) is more apparent. Thus, the Hanafi muftā bihī for cotton-for-yarn is permissibility without equality, based on genus difference.

Key Terms
المحلوج (al-maḥlūj): Combed cotton, with the seeds removed.
الغزل (al-ghazl): Yarn, spun cotton thread.
اختلاف الجنس (ikhtilāf al-jins): Difference in genus or species, which removes the requirement of equality in ribawi items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because they are different genera.
B) No, because they are both cotton products.
C) Only if the quantities are equal.
D) Only if the seller knows the exact amounts.

Key Takeaway
Combed cotton and yarn are different genera, so barter is valid without equality per the relied-upon Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad); however, in mixed transactions with the same genus (e.g., combed for uncombed), the pure component must be greater to avoid riba.

Selling Fresh Dates for Dried Dates: Hadith Dispute and Abu Hanifa's Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on rutab-tamr exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on bartering fresh dates for dried dates, identify the evidence of the majority, and explain Abu Hanifa's rebuttal.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits equal exchange of fresh and dried dates; the majority forbids.

This lesson delves into a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over the permissibility of bartering fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr) in equal measure. While the majority of scholars, including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, prohibit it based on a hadith, Abu Hanifa upholds its validity by questioning the hadith's reliability or interpreting it as applying only to credit sales. Understanding this disagreement illuminates Hanafi methodology in weighing textual evidence against legal principles.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and the dispute
Identify the act (bartering fresh dates for dried dates equally) and the two positions: Abu Hanifa permits, the majority (including Sahibayn) prohibit.
All agree that selling with excess is prohibited.
2
Examine the majority's dalil
Analyze the hadith of Zaid ibn 'Ayash: the Prophet asked 'Does it decrease when it dries?' and upon answer 'Yes' said 'Then no.' Consider its chain and text.
This hadith is narrated by Malik, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, etc.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
Two main arguments: (a) Zaid ibn 'Ayash is a weak narrator, (b) even if the hadith is authentic, it refers to credit sales (nasi'ah) as per the addition in Abu Dawud's narration.
Abu Hanifa also argues that fresh dates are a type of date, so they fall under the general rule of like-for-like.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree with Abu Hanifa. The commentary defends Abu Hanifa's view as mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the rebuttal and the weakness of the opposing hadith.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In the Hanafi school, a spot transaction of fresh dates for dried dates in equal measure is valid; the majority would declare it invalid even if equal.
This affects many transactions in agricultural markets.

قوله : (وهذه الزيادة بعد صحتها (الخ) عبارة الفتح : وأنت تعلم أن بعد صحة هذه الزيادة يجب قبولها لأن المذهب المختار عند المحدثين قبول الزيادة وإن كان الأكثر لم يرووها إلا في زيادة تفرد بها بعض الرواة الحاضرين في مجلس واحد ومثلهم لا يغفل عن مثلها فإنها مردودة على ما كتبناه في تحرير الأصول، وما نحن فيه لم يثبت أنه زيادة لما في مجلس واحد اجتمعوا فيه فسمع هذا ما لم يسمع
His statement: (And fresh dates for fresh dates or for dried dates equally, and grapes for raisins equally). As for the first, it is the statement of Abu Hanifa. And the rest of the scholars, including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, said it is not permissible. And they all agree that selling fresh dates for dried dates with excess is not permissible. The evidence of the majority is his statement when he was asked about it: 'Does it decrease when it dries?' It was said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Then no.' This was narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta and the four [Sunan] from Zaid ibn 'Ayash from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that fresh dates are dried dates because of his statement, peace be upon him, when fresh dates were gifted to him: 'Are all the dates of Khaybar like this?' He called them dates. And it was objected in Ghayat al-Bayan that the gift was dried dates, and he was followed in al-Binaya that what is established in al-Bukhari is that it was dried dates. And because if fresh dates were dried dates, the sale would be permissible by the first part of the hadith (dates for dates), and if not, then by the latter part: 'If the types differ, then sell as you wish.' Thus the Great Imam argued when the scholars of Baghdad gathered against him and were severe towards him for contradicting the report. And he answered their hadith that its chain depends on Zaid ibn 'Ayash, and he is one whose hadith is not accepted. In al-Hidaya: He is weak according to transmitters. And it was objected in al-Binaya that he is trustworthy according to transmitters. Al-Khattabi said: Some people have spoken about the chain of this hadith and said: Zaid ibn 'Ayash is unknown, but it is not so; this Ibn 'Ayash is a client of Banu Zuhrah, and Malik mentioned him in al-Muwatta and narrated his hadith despite his extreme scrutiny of narrators and criticism. And al-Tirmidhi narrated it and said hasan sahih, and Ahmad in his Musnad, Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, and al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak and said: This hadith is sahih due to the consensus of the leading transmitters on Malik's reliability and that he is precise in what he narrates. Al-Hakim said: al-Akmal said: We accept its strength in hadith, but it is a solitary report (khabar wahid) that cannot oppose the well-known. In Ghayat al-Bayan: His statement 'the chain of what they narrated depends on Zaid ibn 'Ayash' and what is mentioned in hadith books is Zaid Abu 'Ayash, and al-Binaya rejected it as a mistake because he is Ibn 'Ayash and his kunya is Abu 'Ayash; similarly, Shaykh 'Ala' al-Din al-Turkumani was mistaken. And the author of al-Tanqih said: Zaid ibn 'Ayash Abu 'Ayash al-Zalani, also called al-Makhzumi, client of Banu Zuhrah, Madani, there is no problem with him. In al-'Inaya: An objection was made that the aforementioned alternation implies the permissibility of selling fried wheat for other fried wheat, because if it is wheat, it is permissible by the first part of the hadith, or if not, by the latter part. Some said this is good speech in debate to repel the opponent's clamor, but the proof is not completed by it, but by what we have explained of applying the name 'dates' to it; it has been established that 'dates' is a name for the fruit that emerges from the palm tree from when its form is set until it ripens, and 'rutab' is a name for a type of it like al-Barni etc. In Fath al-Qadir: He rejected the alternation between its being dates or not by stating that there is a third category: being of the same genus, and it is not permissible to sell it with the other, like fried wheat with other fried wheat because of lack of equality in measure between them. Thus fresh dates and dried dates are not equal in measure; equality only occurs when the two items are in a balanced state, i.e., when the other dries out. And Abu Hanifa forbids that and considers equality at the time of the contract; subsequent decrease does not prevent equality at the time if its cause is a natural matter, i.e., increase in moisture. Unlike fried wheat with other, where we judge non-equality at the time due to one being compacted in measure and the other being loose. And it was also answered regarding the hadith of Zaid ibn 'Ayash that the intended prohibition is for deferred sale (nasi'ah), for it is established in the hadith of Abu 'Ayash this addition of nasi'ah as narrated by Abu Dawud: 'The Messenger of God forbade selling fresh dates for dried dates on credit.' With this wording al-Hakim narrated it and remained silent about it; and al-Tahawi narrated it. This addition, after its authenticity, must be accepted because the chosen position of the muhaddithin is to accept the addition, even if the majority did not narrate it, except an addition that is unique to some attendees in a single sitting; and people like them cannot be negligent of such, so it is rejected. But his statement in that authentic narration 'Do fresh dates decrease when they dry' remains devoid of benefit if the prohibition is for credit. And what they mentioned that its benefit is that fresh dates decrease until the term expires, so there is no benefit in this transaction for the orphan considering the decrease upon drying, so he forbade it out of compassion, is based on the questioner being the guardian of an orphan, and there is no evidence for that. In Sharh al-Tahawi: And if one sells fruits for fruits by estimation, it is not permissible unless it is by measure and he knows their equality in measure before parting bodily.
His statement: (And this addition after its authenticity etc.) is the expression of al-Fath: And you know that after the authenticity of this addition, it must be accepted because the chosen position of the muhaddithin is to accept the addition, even if the majority did not narrate it, except an addition that is unique to some attendees in a single sitting; and people like them cannot be negligent of such, so it is rejected as we have written in Tahrir al-Usul. And our case here is not established as being an addition from a single sitting where they gathered and heard what others did not hear.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage centers on Abu Hanifa's ruling that fresh dates (rutab) may be bartered for dried dates (tamr) in equal measure, and grapes for raisins equally. The majority of scholars, including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, prohibit it. All agree that selling with excess is prohibited. The majority rely on a hadith from Zaid ibn 'Ayash that the Prophet forbade selling fresh dates for dried dates when asked if they decrease upon drying—he said 'then no.' Abu Hanifa counters that the hadith is weak because its narrator Zaid ibn 'Ayash is not reliable; alternatively, he argues that fresh dates are a type of dried date, so the sale falls under the general rule of like-for-like equality. The discussion includes debates about the hadith's authenticity, with some scholars deeming it sahih and others arguing it is a solitary report (khabar wahid) that cannot override established principles. A further response is that the prohibition was only for credit sales (nasi'ah), as per an addition in Abu Dawud's narration. The commentator reviews these arguments, ultimately upholding Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الرطب (al-rutab): Fresh, moist dates just harvested.
التمر (al-tamr): Dried dates.
متماثلاً (mutamāthilan): Equal in measure (by weight or volume) at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school's mufta bihi ruling in this lesson, what is the ruling on selling fresh dates for dried dates in equal measure?
A) It is invalid (fasid) because of the hadith prohibition.
B) It is valid (sahih) if the exchange is immediate and equal at contract.
C) It is makruh because of a weak hadith.
D) It is haram because it inevitably involves riba.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa's view that equal exchange of fresh and dried dates is valid is the operative position, overruling the majority's objection.
